    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer11803534078691343532">A book devoid of genius levels of narration but with the customary gusto of Agatha Christie is still a solid entry in the Hercule Poirot series. <br/><br/>What enhanced the reading experience was the system of chapter allocation. Christie varies her ways of indenting her story from book to book. But this time the chapters were short, with titles of their own.   <span id="freeText11803534078691343532" style="display:none">A book devoid of genius levels of narration but with the customary gusto of Agatha Christie is still a solid entry in the Hercule Poirot series. <br /><br />What enhanced the reading experience was the system of chapter allocation. Christie varies her ways of indenting her story from book to book. But this time the chapters were short, with titles of their own. I liked that.<br /><br />There was no stupefying twists present but the murderer...well I don't want to spoil the fun for you. Altogether the translators keep on changing but the quality remains there or thereabouts.<br /><br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2941370345" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2941370345" />
    <a title="Bionic Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean"><img alt="Bionic Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586709362p2/19300421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2941370345?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bionic Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bionic Jean" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean">Bionic Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781444802498" href="/book/show/34068971-lord-edgware-dies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=read-authors-c-d">read-authors-c-d</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=mystery-crime">mystery-crime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2941370345" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5638274823607478654">Which is a better title, in your opinion: <b>Lord Edgware Dies</b> or <i>“Thirteen at Dinner”</i>? It depends, you might say. When does Lord Edgware Die? Is it a spoiler? And what is the significance of the number thirteen, at dinner?<br/><br/>Well to have thirteen guests at dinner is, according to superstition, unlucky. The legend goes that if thirteen are present at a dinner, then bad luck will come to the person who first leaves the table. And, yes Lord Edgware does die, although not before the story starts, so that</span>
  <span id="freeText5638274823607478654" style="display:none">Which is a better title, in your opinion: <b>Lord Edgware Dies</b> or <i>“Thirteen at Dinner”</i>? It depends, you might say. When does Lord Edgware Die? Is it a spoiler? And what is the significance of the number thirteen, at dinner?<br /><br />Well to have thirteen guests at dinner is, according to superstition, unlucky. The legend goes that if thirteen are present at a dinner, then bad luck will come to the person who first leaves the table. And, yes Lord Edgware does die, although not before the story starts, so that the entire novel is a “flashback”, but <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ in chapter 5 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Neither does he die at the end of the story: it is a true whodunnit, where the rule is that enough clues are provided as the story moves along, for the reader to be able to solve it. <br /><br />(I did not by the way. As usual I was merrily carried away by the fact that I had spotted a detail near the beginning, which would explain the otherwise hidden identity of the murderer. But no. It was a red herring, carefully placed by Agatha Christie to entrap and misdirect innocent readers like me.)<br /><br />In fact there is a lot of skilful misdirection in <b>Lord Edgware Dies</b>. It is well up to standard for a Poirot novel. And it also has a little welcome humour, such as this comment by one of the characters, where the author delightfully pokes fun at herself:<br /><br /><i>“And that very same evening – that very same evening – Lord Edgware dies. Good title that, by the way. Lord Edgware Dies. Look well on a book stall.”</i><br /><br />So why the two titles? It came as a surprise to me, to learn that this novel by an English author was originally published in the USA. It was entitled <i>“Thirteen for Dinner”</i>, and serialised in six monthly parts, between March and August 1933, in <i>“The American Magazine”</i>. Just a month later, in September 1933, it was published in the UK as <b>Lord Edgware Dies</b>, and we know it better now under that later title. But I feel there should be a warning to Agatha Christie enthusiasts, who may believe that they have stumbled on a lesser known work by the Great Dame: they are the same novel. And interestingly, both titles do actually function as a kind of spoiler, although it is not really possible to tell until the conclusion is known.<br /><br />And the dedication? Who were <i>“Dr. and Mrs. Campbell Thompson”</i>?<br /><br />At the time of writing, the author and her second husband, Max Mallowan, the archaeologist, were friends with the Campbell Thompson couple. Agatha Christie and Max Mallowan had first met at the Ur site three years previously, in 1930, and married the same year. Reginald Campbell Thompson had employed Max to work on a dig at the ancient Assyrian city of Upper Mesopotamia, located on the outskirts of Mosul in modern-day northern Iraq. <br /><br />Apparently Thompson was notoriously tight-fisted; so much so that he wanted Agatha Christie to use use orange boxes as a desk! Agatha Christie instead insisted on buying a solid table to place her typewriter on, in order to complete her next book. Thompson was aghast at the fact that she spent ten pounds of her own money on a table at a local bazaar, and apparently took two weeks to recover his temper over such an “extravagance”! The book in question was, of course, <b>Lord Edgware Dies</b>. <br /><br />Despite these disagreements, Thompson often asked Agatha how the book was progressing. In fact the Thompson couple were uniquely honoured, because Agatha Christie read the manuscript of the book aloud to them, and this was something that she only ever usually did for her family. Agatha Christie and Reginald Campbell Thompson clearly regained their mutual respect, because not only did she dedicate the novel to the Thompson couple, but a skeleton found on the dig was named <i>“Lord Edgware”</i>!<br /><br />There is little point in retelling the story here. There are plenty of places where one can read a synopsis of this convoluted tale. As you would expect, Agatha Christie’s ninth work featuring her great detective Hercule Poirot, shows her well into her stride. Satisfyingly, both Hercule Poirot and Captain Hastings are present for the whole story, rather than one of them making a late appearance, as sometimes happens. Once again, Hastings’s wife, Dulcie Duveen, or <i>“Cinderella”</i>, first introduced to us in <i>“Murder on the Links”</i>, does not appear in the novel. Presumably she is back at home on their ranch in the Argentine. The single reference to this comes at the beginning of the last chapter, when:<br /><br /><i>“I was suddenly recalled to the Argentine”</i>.<br /><br />Chief Inspector Japp too, is also on the scene, as the officer in change of the murder case – or cases, I should say – as there will be three murders in total.<br /><br />For much of the novel we watch the interaction between these three. The relationship between Poirot and Hastings is far better described than in the previous novel, <i>“Peril at End House”</i> in which the duo seemed to spend all their time carping at one another. The author Robert Barnard has noticed this too, calling the novel:<br /><br /><i>“clever and unusual, with the Hastings/Poirot relationship done less crudely than usual.”</i><br /><br />The narrator is as ever, Captain Arthur Hastings, who describes what he thinks of as his friend’s limitations in an affectionate way:<br /><br /><i>“Oh! I know very well that you always have a little idea that I am conceited, but, indeed, I assure you, I am really a very humble person.”<br />I laughed.<br />“You – humble!”<br />“It is so. Except – I confess it – that I am a little proud of my moustaches.”</i><br /><br />And Poirot kindly observes to Hastings:<br /><br /><i>“No human being should learn from another. Each individual should develop his own powers to the uttermost, not try to imitate those of someone else. I do not wish you to be a second and inferior Poirot. I wish you to be the supreme Hastings. And you are the supreme Hastings. In you, Hastings, I find the normal mind almost perfectly illustrated.”</i><br /><br />Hastings is also quick to observe that Inspector Japp does not usually have Poirot’s powers of deduction and insight. Nevertheless he reports events faithfully. Here is an exchange between Poirot and the good Inspector:<br /><br /><i>“You have the confidence — always the confidence! You never stop and say to yourself — can it be so? You never doubt — or wonder. You never think: this is too easy!’<br />'You bet your life I don’t! And that's just where, if you’ll excuse me saying so, you go off the rails every time. Why shouldn’t a thing be easy? What’s the harm in a thing being easy?’”</i><br /><br />The trio are a treat to read about, and their little peccadilloes only add to the enjoyment of this classic golden age mystery.<br /><br />It is obvious from the title that the characters are going to be aristocrats and those in high society. We move in these circles throughout the novel, and also into the realms of the theatre. Lord Edgware’s wife is the actress, Jane Wilkinson, and we plunge straight into the nitty-gritty of the book when, at the end of chapter one, she announces:<br /><br /><i>“M. Poirot, somehow or other I’ve just got to get rid of my husband.”</i><br /><br />We know the next bit of the plot, from the title, but who exactly might have done the deadly deed? Apart from his wife, there are other suspects within his family. There is his nephew, Captain Ronald Marsh, who is the heir to his title, and has money troubles. There is Geraldine Marsh, his daughter from his first marriage, whose life he makes a misery. There is the Duke of Merton, whom Lord Edgware’s wife wishes to be free to marry. And as ever, there are butlers, housekeepers and maids of the various households, all of whom could easily have a good motive to murder Lord Edgware.<br /><br />Prominent in the story are Jane Wilkinson, who like many actresses depicted in fiction, seems completely self-obsessed, and described by her friends as having no conscience at all. But despite reports of having been seen in Lord Edgware’s house at the time of the murder, she has a perfect alibi, as she was also seen attending a dinner with a dozen other guests.<br /><br />We also meet Carlotta Adams, an American impersonator, who is on tour in London and Paris. In a performance watched by Poirot and Hastings in chapter 1, she mimics Jane Wilkinson exceptionally well. Interestingly, this performance is viewed with pleasure by Jane Wilkinson herself, who is seated in the audience, just behind Poirot and Hastings.<br /><br />Surely the inclusion of a talented impersonator, who is able to mimic one of the chief suspects, cannot be immaterial to the plot? Throughout much of the novel, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and even after the murder of Carlotta Adams  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> we are trying to work out what exactly might have happened here. Carlotta Adams herself is an endearing character. She was based on the American dramatist Ruth Draper, and Agatha Christie was so impressed by her, that she became the inspiration behind the story. In Agatha Christie’s autobiography, she wrote:<br /><br /><i>“I thought how clever she was and how good her impersonations were; the wonderful way she could transform herself from a nagging wife to a peasant girl kneeling in a cathedral. Thinking about her led me to the book <b>Lord Edgware Dies</b>”</i><br /><br />The characters in <b>Lord Edgware Dies</b> are slightly better filled out than in some of her other novels. Another feisty female is Genevieve or “Jenny” Driver, a friend of Carlotta Adams, who specialises in the creation of fashionable hats. Another strong character is Bryan Martin, a successful actor, who knows all three females well. Other actors and minor members of the aristocracy come and go.<br /><br />To say the settings are so limited – various barely described locations in London – it remains an absorbing read. Much of the text consists of dialogue between the detectives and those involved in the case, and even more is between Poirot and Hastings, where Poirot explains his thoughts (or Hastings complains that he doesn’t!) We are privy to all Poirot’s mistaken theories, as he postulates first one solution, and then another. But none will suffice, as none cover all the questions Poirot has asked himself. This infuriates Japp, who merely looks for the obvious answer, and we gleefully watch the irritable conversations that ensue.<br /><br />The structure of the story is also nicely done. Right at the end of the book is an account, a letter to Poirot <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ written by the murderer, confirming the clever mind at work behind the rather dim affected personality displayed to the public <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. It is a satisfying way to end the novel. And the opposite end of the book, the conclusion to chapter one already quoted, sets a precedent for many of the other chapter endings to also end with a surprise — or on a cliffhanger —to ensure that we carry on reading. <br /><br />Red herrings are placed randomly, some of which we pick up, certain that this time they will not be misdirection, but actual clues which we have cleverly spotted. (We are invariably wrong!) A person in a particular place at a particular time. An initial which is the correct one. A relationship which had been hidden hitherto, but is suddenly revealed. Surely these are pertinent?<br /><br />Probably not. Things are never as they seem.<br /><br /><i>“Enemies! People these days don’t have enemies! Not English people!”<br /><br />“Do you not know, my friend, that each one of us is a dark mystery, a maze of conflicting passions and desires and aptitudes? Mais oui, c’est vrai. One makes one’s little judgments — but nine times out of ten one is wrong.”<br /><br />“One cannot be interested in crime without being interested in psychology. It is not the mere act of killing, it is what lies behind it that appeals to the expert.”</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="5638274823607478654" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_968733007" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/968733007" />
    <a title="Jeff " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12618229-jeff"><img alt="Jeff " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529089518p2/12618229.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/968733007?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff " href="/user/show/12618229-jeff">Jeff </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/4020551-thirteen-at-dinner">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12618229-jeff?shelf=who-done-it">who-done-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer968733007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18418388815518276703">Take it from me, nothing is as <i>"riveting"</i> as Hercules Poirot hanging about letting the little grey cells percolate.<br/><br/>If you’re looking for the master detective to bitch slap a Duke or put the moves on some English babe or pull out a gat and plug a few holes in the snobbish butler, move on dear reader. Poirot thinks. A lot.<br/><br/>In this volume, he does lots and lots of contemplating and not much in the way of getting about and searching for clues. Let that pompous fool, Inspector Japp, do most of the leg</span>
  <span id="freeText18418388815518276703" style="display:none">Take it from me, nothing is as <i>"riveting"</i> as Hercules Poirot hanging about letting the little grey cells percolate.<br><br>If you’re looking for the master detective to bitch slap a Duke or put the moves on some English babe or pull out a gat and plug a few holes in the snobbish butler, move on dear reader. Poirot thinks. A lot.<br><br>In this volume, he does lots and lots of contemplating and not much in the way of getting about and searching for clues. Let that pompous fool, Inspector Japp, do most of the leg work while Poirot takes a nap or eats le omelet. “Hastings, my dear friend, we haven’t gone over the clues in a page and a half, let us do so once again.”<br><br>Again, I don’t expect Poirot to chase a suspect, leaping across London rooftops in a single bound or beat a knife wielding thug into a bloody pulp or hang out at the hotel pool, drinking Boilermakers and ogling the honeys, but in this volume, our Belgian detective is more inert than usual.<br><br>Plus, this very regular mystery, doesn’t offer a lot of mental gymnastics for the reader looking for a brain workout. The answer is pretty much there after a quarter of the book, then Christie plays hide the suspect salami, diverting the reader away from the truth with feeble feints and jabs. The fun for these types of mysteries is having to re-adjust your thinking cap when different clues and suspects are brought to light – this book just brought on le brain cramps.<br><br>If you want better Christie or Poirot, check out <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16328.The_Murder_of_Roger_Ackroyd__Hercule_Poirot__4_" title="The Murder of Roger Ackroyd (Hercule Poirot #4) by Agatha Christie" rel="nofollow noopener">The Murder of Roger Ackroyd</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/853510.Murder_on_the_Orient_Express__Hercule_Poirot___10_" title="Murder on the Orient Express (Hercule Poirot, #10) by Agatha Christie" rel="nofollow noopener">Murder on the Orient Express</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16322.The_A_B_C__Murders__Hercule_Poirot__13_" title="The A.B.C. Murders (Hercule Poirot #13) by Agatha Christie" rel="nofollow noopener">The A.B.C. Murders</a>.<br><br>Oh, and the American title of this book makes very little sense in the grand scheme of things, I would have stuck with the original title, <u>Lord Edgeware Dies</u>. It’s what this perfunctory mystery deserves.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528805647i/25731544.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_2866300025" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2866300025?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evgeny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evgeny" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny">Evgeny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=hercule-poirot">hercule-poirot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=crime-and-mystery">crime-and-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2866300025" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5556244215714182531">Incomparable (according to himself) Hercule Poirot overheard a famous American actress saying she would love to kill her husband. Lo and behold, some time later the guy really was killed. The suspect was sort of obvious in the beginning, only it turned out not only the actress has absolutely no motive for doing the deed, but it was physically impossible for her to do as she was at a dinner with twelve other people all the time (thus another titles of the book: Thirteen at Dinner). <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1562028726i/27753358._SX540_.jpg' alt='Dinner' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>The problem in</span>
  <span id="freeText5556244215714182531" style="display:none">Incomparable (according to himself) Hercule Poirot overheard a famous American actress saying she would love to kill her husband. Lo and behold, some time later the guy really was killed. The suspect was sort of obvious in the beginning, only it turned out not only the actress has absolutely no motive for doing the deed, but it was physically impossible for her to do as she was at a dinner with twelve other people all the time (thus another titles of the book: Thirteen at Dinner). <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1562028726i/27753358._SX540_.jpg" alt="Dinner" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The problem interested Poirot and so the great detective jumped into investigation to the delight of his faithful friend and sidekick Capt. Hastings. Much to Hastings' chagrin Poirot's investigation consisted of sitting in a comfortable chair excising his famed grey cells. <br><br>On the negative side the story was narrated by Capt. Hastings. By this book of the series he became bland. Also while in the previous books he demonstrated some (rare, but still) heightening of his intellect, here he was completely hopelessly stupid. No wonder he stops being the narrator in the next books of the series. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1562029049i/27753366._SX540_.jpg" alt="Forrest Gump" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>No, he was not even stupid in a cute way like Forrest Gump.<br><br>On the positive side I know exactly one person in real life who was able to solve the mystery before Poirot explained it. The guy's reasoning was simply, "I do not like this person's face, thus I know the murderer". In other words, it was a lucky guess. Nobody else could guess the identity of the culprit despite the fact that readers are given all the clues Poirot had. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1562029049i/27753367._SX540_.jpg" alt="Clue" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>In my book this is the definition of a good mystery.<br><br>So while narration was not great and Poirot spent most of his time sitting on his behind reflecting on what was going on the mystery itself was top-notch with plenty of red herrings and plot twists. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1562029049i/27753368._SX540_.png" alt="Mystery" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>4 stars is my rating. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5556244215714182531" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3502088121" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3502088121" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/100387236-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567614515p2/100387236.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3502088121?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/100387236-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007120741" href="/book/show/121647.Lord_Edgware_Dies">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3502088121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12069797776813266338">My recent Agatha Christie reads were not remarkable. However this book has made me realise why I used to like Agatha Christie's work so much. This is a Poirot mystery in which Poirot isn't overly arrogant and irritating and the plot is intriguing enough.<br/><br/>Jane Wilkinson a beautiful actress is married to Lord Edgware. One evening at a hotel, she walks over to Poirot and Hastings in the middle of dinner, to have a private conversation. She tells Poirot that she is in love with a Duke and wants to di</span>
  <span id="freeText12069797776813266338" style="display:none">My recent Agatha Christie reads were not remarkable. However this book has made me realise why I used to like Agatha Christie's work so much. This is a Poirot mystery in which Poirot isn't overly arrogant and irritating and the plot is intriguing enough.<br /><br />Jane Wilkinson a beautiful actress is married to Lord Edgware. One evening at a hotel, she walks over to Poirot and Hastings in the middle of dinner, to have a private conversation. She tells Poirot that she is in love with a Duke and wants to divorce her husband and if he doesn't oblige, she might have to kill him (half joking, I guess)! She wants Poirot to meet Lord Edgware and convince him to grant her divorce. Reluctantly Poirot complies to this strange request. <br /><br />Upon meeting Lord Edgware, Poirot learns that he had already send a letter to Jane stating that he has no objection in granting her divorce. Poirot confronts Jane and she says she didn't receive any letter, but is happy that her wish is granted and yes, she needn't murder him! But as fate may have it, Lord Edgware is found murdered in his home and his butler and maid thinks it's Jane who has done the deed. Now it's upto Poirot and Hastings to figure out this mess.<br /><br />I enjoyed this book a lot even though I could find a few plot holes in it which I will list in the spoilers section. Reccomend this book to all Agatha Christie mystery lovers.<br /><br />**Spoilers**<br /><br />Points which I found unconvincing:<br />1. The Duke falls in love with Jane knowing that she is a married woman. The only way he can marry her is if her husband is dead.<br />Since that is not a choice, he should have stopped pursuing this relationship. Since he doesn't, does that mean he is indirectly involved in the murder?<br /><br />2. The whole story is a bit unusual. To find a voice actress who can imitate Jane is understandable. But a whole bunch of people at a party is unable to recognise her (she is a famous actress) is hard to believe! No matter how good Carlotta Adams is at impersonation, this is strange.<br /><br />3. The book doesn't explain how Jane is there at the right moment to kill Ross, just before Poirot meets up with him.<br /><br />Nevertheless it was a fun read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12069797776813266338" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/5837456-susan">Susan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/35618439-lord-edgware-dies">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2543017440" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3568530187892586104">American actress, Jane Wilkinson, Lady Edgware, approaches Poirot, asking him to help her obtain a divorce from her cold, estranged husband. However, when Poirot, and Hastings, go to see Lord Edgware, he seems to have no issue with divorcing her. It seems that Jane Wilkinson, after all, will have her freedom and then the probable becomes the definite, when Lord Edgware is found dead. <br/><br/>This is not my favourite Poirot novel, although it is always delightful to have Hastings and Japp both involved i</span>
  <span id="freeText3568530187892586104" style="display:none">American actress, Jane Wilkinson, Lady Edgware, approaches Poirot, asking him to help her obtain a divorce from her cold, estranged husband. However, when Poirot, and Hastings, go to see Lord Edgware, he seems to have no issue with divorcing her. It seems that Jane Wilkinson, after all, will have her freedom and then the probable becomes the definite, when Lord Edgware is found dead. <br /><br />This is not my favourite Poirot novel, although it is always delightful to have Hastings and Japp both involved in a mystery. There are a good cast of suspects and twists in the tale, as Poirot uncovers who really killed Lord Edgware, and why. There are mercenary actresses, female impersonators, a downtrodden daughter, a ne’er do well nephew, alibis, and twists galore. <br /><br />Hugh Fraser narrates this delightfully – I am a huge fan of his reading of Audible books. The next in the series is Murder on the Orient Express, and I intend to listen to David Suchet narrating one of my very favourite Poirot mysteries. Poirot remains my favourite, fictional detective and it is a delight to revisit the books. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3568530187892586104" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2190530619" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2190530619" />
    <a title="Cyndi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7132123-cyndi"><img alt="Cyndi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510671513p2/7132123.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2190530619?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cyndi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cyndi" href="/user/show/7132123-cyndi">Cyndi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2190530619" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11382349230082074040">So, as the title says, Lord Edgeware dies. His wife tells everyone she’s going to kill him and how. But when he does get killed she has an air tight alibi, even though she was seen at the scene of the crime. It’s up to Poirot to find the true murderer. I don’t think I’ve read a Poirot book before where he says, “I was wrong!” so many times. This one stumped him for quite awhile. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer15525986763618543486">3.5 stars. I actually changed my mind about this book right at the end, during Poirot’s gathering people together to reveal the murderer. I found I did not like the characters involved in the case, and this is the first time I’ve found Hastings genuinely tiresome. Poirot chastising Hastings for being so simple-minded throughout the book had me wholeheartedly agreeing. <br/>Then, Poirot put one of the suspects through the wringer because the suspect had dared to tell Poirot a series of really dumb sto</span>
  <span id="freeText15525986763618543486" style="display:none">3.5 stars. I actually changed my mind about this book right at the end, during Poirot’s gathering people together to reveal the murderer. I found I did not like the characters involved in the case, and this is the first time I’ve found Hastings genuinely tiresome. Poirot chastising Hastings for being so simple-minded throughout the book had me wholeheartedly agreeing. <br />Then, Poirot put one of the suspects through the wringer because the suspect had dared to tell Poirot a series of really dumb stories. That showed a side of Poirot I had not seen before, a rather frightening side I can well imagine he used to advantage when he worked in Belgium years before. And the reveal of the murderer and an explanation of the murderer’s devious plan, which I’d seen parts of and had even briefly suspected this person, was really smart. Well done, Mrs. Christie!</span>

<div id="review_2430827163" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2430827163" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2430827163?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007120741" href="/book/show/121647.Lord_Edgware_Dies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=classic-mystery">classic-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2430827163" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13837103791605189863">This is also cleverly executed plot by Agatha Christie. <br/>Lord Edgware, an unpopular aristocrat, is murdered in his home. And who is the initial suspect? His wife, as she wanted to divorce him and remarry. But is she really the culprit? Isn’t there any other who would have wanted him dead, like his nephew, the next in line to the title, who is in the midst of financial crisis? Question after question rises as Christie takes us through the murder mystery where Poirot is dealing with a clever and ca</span>
  <span id="freeText13837103791605189863" style="display:none">This is also cleverly executed plot by Agatha Christie. <br />Lord Edgware, an unpopular aristocrat, is murdered in his home. And who is the initial suspect? His wife, as she wanted to divorce him and remarry. But is she really the culprit? Isn’t there any other who would have wanted him dead, like his nephew, the next in line to the title, who is in the midst of financial crisis? Question after question rises as Christie takes us through the murder mystery where Poirot is dealing with a clever and calculating murderer. However, the murderer finally succumbs to Poirot's brilliant analytical and deductive powers, but not without mistakes from the part of the great detective. <br /><br />I did like this new approach of bringing out mistaken "little ideas" of Poirot in to light. One feels that this brilliant detective is after all human and do make mistakes too, and not a superhuman. <br /><br />As I said before, it is a well executed point. Even though by the mid of the story, the reader can guess the murderer, Christie with her usual twists and turns keeps of keeping the reader in doubt as to whether the guess is correct. Also Christie had the ability to create plots that are thematically similar yet different in their execution. Only an exceptionally gifted writer can do that. <br /><br />However, despite all the plus points of the story, the pace was rather slow and I found our brilliant detective a little too inert. Perhaps, like Mr. Hastings, I'm becoming impatient and fond of action. But no matter, comparatively this story lacked that gripping effect I have enjoyed in many a Poirot books. It is a good read, overall, but not one of Christie's best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13837103791605189863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/21938800-john">John</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer10467535381467604436">One of my favorite Poiret mysteries. <br/><br/>SPOILER ALERT: if you do not want to know who the murderer is do not continue reading this review. <br/><br/>Jane Wilkinson announces in front of several witnesses that she is going to get a taxi and go and kill her husband Lord Edgware.   <span id="freeText10467535381467604436" style="display:none">One of my favorite Poiret mysteries. <br /><br />SPOILER ALERT: if you do not want to know who the murderer is do not continue reading this review. <br /><br />Jane Wilkinson announces in front of several witnesses that she is going to get a taxi and go and kill her husband Lord Edgware. A day later he is murdered. So many suspects. His Greek godlike butler, his daughter Geraldine who hates him, Roland his pecuniary challenged nephew, the efficient secretary, Bryan Adams an actor jilted by Jane or perhaps the Duke of Merton’d mother or the Duke. Throw in Carlotta an American actress and you have a wealth of choices. <br /><br />Lady Edgware is a beautiful woman with one goal in mind to marry the Catholic Duke and become a Duchess. The beauty of this plot is that she is absolutely ruthless. Kills the Duke with her maids corn knife, poisons Carlotta with veronal and eliminates the hapless Ross when he tumbled she was not at the dinner party which provides her alibi. <br /><br />Hastings is marvelous as Poiret’s sidekick and Japp just wants to arrest someone without to much evidence. The key is Ellis her maid and it is through her Poiret answers his five questions. <br /><br />Why did Lord Edgware change his mind about the divorce?<br />What happened to the letter he sent agreeing to divorce?<br />What was the meaning of the furious expression on his face that Hastings saw when leaving the library?<br />Why were the pince-nez glasses in Carlotta’s bag?<br />Why did someone telephone Jane Wilkinson at the Chiswick dinner part and who was it?<br /><br />I think Lady Edgware deserves to be in Madame Tussaud’s. She was a wonderful amoral character without conscience and utterly ruthless.</span>

<div id="review_394010961" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/394010961" />
    <a title="Geoffrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11940923-geoffrey"><img alt="Geoffrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345160241p2/11940923.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/394010961?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Geoffrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Geoffrey" href="/user/show/11940923-geoffrey">Geoffrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer394010961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5889033833626510566">This is probably my second favorite Agatha Christie novel, right behind "The Murder of Roger Ackroyd." It also has a special place in my heart because it was the first Agatha Christie novel that I actually read. This book made me want to actually read more Christie novels instead of merely watching the film and/or television adaptations. Generally speaking, I love "Lord Edgeware Dies" because I immensely enjoy all of the elements of the book. First, I found the story itself very engaging. Specif</span>
  <span id="freeText5889033833626510566" style="display:none">This is probably my second favorite Agatha Christie novel, right behind "The Murder of Roger Ackroyd." It also has a special place in my heart because it was the first Agatha Christie novel that I actually read. This book made me want to actually read more Christie novels instead of merely watching the film and/or television adaptations. Generally speaking, I love "Lord Edgeware Dies" because I immensely enjoy all of the elements of the book. First, I found the story itself very engaging. Specifically, the tale maintained my interest from the opening pages right until the conclusion. The twists and turns within the story, especially those regarding the murder case itself, kept me engaged in the narrative and made me want to see how the entire tale would end. I also loved the novel's characterization. I think that Agatha Christie does a nice job of representing each character in this story as a distinctive and interesting personality. Finally, I enjoy the solution to this particular mystery because I think that it is brilliant. In fact, this solution is one of my personal favorites among those in the Christie canon. Thus, because of the aforementioned elements, "Lord Edgeware Dies" ranks high on my list of favorite Christies.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2936403851" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2936403851" />
    <a title="Stephanie Anze" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27117957-stephanie-anze"><img alt="Stephanie Anze" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604709893p2/27117957.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2936403851?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie Anze" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie Anze" href="/user/show/27117957-stephanie-anze">Stephanie Anze</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27117957-stephanie-anze?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2936403851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4723446993716881070">“Do you know my friend that each one of us is a dark mystery, a maze of conflicting passions and desire and aptitudes?” <br/><br/>Hercule Poirot is approached by one Lady Edgware, who now goes by her maiden name Jane Wilkinson as she is separated from her husband. Jane Wilkinson is in love and wants to get married again but first she needs Lord Edgware to grant her a divorce. She asks Poirot to persuade him do so or else she is going to have to kill her husband. Poirot sees Lord Edgware, he assures him th</span>
  <span id="freeText4723446993716881070" style="display:none">“Do you know my friend that each one of us is a dark mystery, a maze of conflicting passions and desire and aptitudes?” <br /><br />Hercule Poirot is approached by one Lady Edgware, who now goes by her maiden name Jane Wilkinson as she is separated from her husband. Jane Wilkinson is in love and wants to get married again but first she needs Lord Edgware to grant her a divorce. She asks Poirot to persuade him do so or else she is going to have to kill her husband. Poirot sees Lord Edgware, he assures him that he will grant the divorce. The next day Lord Edgware is dead. Jane was attending a dinner party that night where she was seen. Who then, killed Lord Edgware?<br /><br />Another great book by Agatha Christie. After seeing a performance by Carlotta Adams, an impressionist, Poirot is approached by Jane Wilkinson. She has been separated from Lord Edgware for a couple of years and now want to remarry. She says he does not want ot give her a divorce and makes a bold statement: if he does not, she will have to kill him. Poirot does not know what to believe and acquaintances near her assure him she speaks her mind like this all the time. Poirot, along with Hastings, pays a visit to Lord Edgware. He says he already has agreed to give Jane a divorce. Poirot delivers the news to Jane. The next day, Lord Edgware is dead, Jane has an alibi and Poirot feels like something is terribly amiss. With a number of suspects and red herrings, this was another delightful murder-mystery. Lord Edgware had others with grudges against him other than his wife. Initially the case gets the better of Poirot as he becomes befuddled. One comment made by a stranger, gets his little grey cells working and Poirot puts all the pieces of this murder together. Clever and fun, this was a most enjoyable book.<br /><br />Originally published in 1933, this is the ninth installment in the Poirot series. Initially, the novel was serialized across six issues of The American Magazine under the title of <i>Thirteen at Dinner</i>. Agatha Christie wrote a bulk of this book while on an excavation trip with her husband Max Mallowan. Christie was inspired to write this book after seeing a performance by Ruth Draper, an American dramatist. This tome was one of the first to become a film. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4723446993716881070" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780062073891" href="/book/show/10183005-lord-edgware-dies">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=mystery-crime">mystery-crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=1930s">1930s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2322536352" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16096728995561978314">“And the same evening – the very same evening – Lord Edgware dies. Good title that, by the way. Lord Edgware Dies. Look well on a bookstall.”<br/><br/>I get the impression Christie had a lot of fun with this one. It is a very plot twist heavy book, with every couple of chapters presenting a new clue or red herring that changes your perception of events. I’m rather pleased to say I solved this one, but figuring out who did it did not hurt it in the slightest. This is one of those mystery novels where I del</span>
  <span id="freeText16096728995561978314" style="display:none">“And the same evening – the very same evening – Lord Edgware dies. Good title that, by the way. Lord Edgware Dies. Look well on a bookstall.”<br /><br />I get the impression Christie had a lot of fun with this one. It is a very plot twist heavy book, with every couple of chapters presenting a new clue or red herring that changes your perception of events. I’m rather pleased to say I solved this one, but figuring out who did it did not hurt it in the slightest. This is one of those mystery novels where I delighted in just watching events unfold and seeing the awareness come to others.<br /><br />The plot follows Poirot as he meets an actress desperate to be rid of her husband Lord Edgware. She asks Poirot to convince Edgware to grant her a divorce, but not after loudly announcing that she would be happy just to go to their house and flat out kill him. Poirot investigates the matter and finds that getting Edgware to agree is shockingly easy… then the next day he is murdered, and it becomes a less than easy task at figuring out who killed Lord Edgware, especially considering that that the obvious suspect no longer had a reason for wishing his demise. <br /><br />This is another Hastings narrated novel and another one where he didn’t annoy me. It seems like around Peril at End House, Christie decided to tone down his stupidity and while still making him naïve, gave him a bit more witty dialogue. There are a couple of moments where he notes Poirot’s arrogance in a rather brilliant manner and plays off of his sense of superiority towards others. There is also one wonderful scene where Christie seems to be trying to explain why she feels he is an important character. As Poirot explains to him; “You are beautifully and perfectly balanced. In you sanity is personified. Do you realize what that means to me? When the criminal sets out to do a crime his first effort is to deceive. Who does he seek to deceive? The image in his mind is that of the normal man. There is probably no such thing actually – it is a mathematical abstraction. But you come as near to realizing it as possible.”<br /><br />In classic Poirot fashion his compliments are more than slightly insulting… and what can I say, I still find that his personality is what keeps me coming back. He is that annoying person who feels he’s the smartest man in the room and has to show it off to everyone… in Poirot’s case though, he’s right.<br /><br />As I said, I solved the case in this one before Poirot did, but that in no way hurt the novel. After hitting a streak of some decent novels in the series, we again hit a something special. Lord Edgware Dies receives four out of five stars and a strong recommendation for all mystery fans. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16096728995561978314" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1726559354" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1726559354" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1726559354?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=mystery-detective-thriller">mystery-detective-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1726559354" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14399772064830260386">I have never until recently been a mystery reader, so I kind of have a meta-view of them, in a way. I think: what is the attraction to this kind of tightly plotted tale? Maybe it gives us comfort that seemingly intractable problems like global warming can, with a little pluck and luck and Reason and Order, get figured out in time for tea! If we are in the hands of guys like Poirot, we will be JUST FINE.<br/><br/>Anyway, so in this one the problem is seemingly straightforward: Lord Edgware Dies. Surprise! </span>
  <span id="freeText14399772064830260386" style="display:none">I have never until recently been a mystery reader, so I kind of have a meta-view of them, in a way. I think: what is the attraction to this kind of tightly plotted tale? Maybe it gives us comfort that seemingly intractable problems like global warming can, with a little pluck and luck and Reason and Order, get figured out in time for tea! If we are in the hands of guys like Poirot, we will be JUST FINE.<br /><br />Anyway, so in this one the problem is seemingly straightforward: Lord Edgware Dies. Surprise! The title used to be Thirteen at Dinner, which is, let’s say, a bit drier and more subtle. But this sort of thing gets at Christie’s more direct, unfussy approach. Sometimes she has chapter titles such as “Mostly Discussion,” or “Poirot Speaks.” They in a way seem like placeholder titles, as if she were drafting the chapters, still, but I think they are more about her straight-on way of proceeding. Though I have to say, Hastings tells this tale, and he is rather flat and unimaginative. Maybe that’s what titles like that entail: comic cluelessness. But I like the glibness of that, too. <br /><br />Here is an example of Hastings at his comical best:<br /><br />After one woman dies of an apparent drug overdose, Hastings, who is Poirot’s actual assistant (!?), observes, “But she seemed like a thoroughly nice girl!” What did you not see, M. Hastings?! I mean, we are clueless, too, but he makes us feel a little less dumb than we might otherwise feel.<br /><br />Poirot at one point says all people were given brains by God to figure things like criminal detection out, but he wonders if God may have passed Hastings by when He was passing out the brains. We laugh, even as we fail to understand what it is Hastings doesn’t quite get.<br /><br />And though I am somewhat left of socialism politically, I still seem to like the fussy egg-headed Belgian ex-retiree detective Poirot (pronounced Pwah-Row), even in his amusing adoration of royalty. I appreciate the fact that in 1933, as fascism looms in Europe, his greatest anxiety occurs when he spills a bit of omelet on his vest at brunch.<br /><br />Again with the anyway, though I am on record for hating Hastings, Pwah-Row’s Watson-like sidekick, by now I am getting used to his dimwittedness. It’s interesting to have an almost completely obtuse narrator; thus we can keep the narrative rolling as Poirot keeps secrets about what he knows, and when Hastings tries to analyze what is going on, we can trust he is wrong every time, as is Scotland Yard Inspector Zapp. Use your little grey cells, boys! The easiest and most obvious solution to the problem is never the right one! But you know Poirot WILL persevere, and in the process teach us to think harder. Harder than Hastings, at least.<br /><br />The attractions of this one include 1) the fact that Lord Edgware has “interests” (never detailed, sorry) in S &amp; M (which means he has to die, kids, duh, he’s a bad ’ne); 2) a confession letter, shared only AFTER Poirot solves the damned thing, written from prison; 3) the fact that Lady Edgware comes to Poirot and tells him she wants his help in getting rid of her husband, this on the day before he dies; 4) Christie’s continuing interest in plays, acting, which of course makes sense because of her interest in crime, in subterfuge. But she has by this time seen her books adapted for screen and stage, and has started to write plays herself. She's been meeting actors, getting to know them. Perfect murderers, they can lie to your face and get away with it. Two actresses are part of this story, too, though they are not the only ones engaging in “bearing false witness,” shall we say.<br /><br />One small amusing thing: One guy talks to Poirot about being trailed everywhere by a man with a gold tooth. Poirot calls bullshit on this, remarking that people never have gold teeth anymore! Especially not in America! Well, times change.<br /><br />This is a good one, in the upper echelon of his works so far, I say, as I proceed through all of them. Classic Christie! I liked it a lot. And no, I never figured it out along the way; I never do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14399772064830260386" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16896763353465638403" style="display:none">Okay, so when you read a book within the Hercule Poirot Series you know you're going to get one thing and one thing only: a mysterious murder. However, the title of this book gives away who the victim is - Lord Edgware. <br /><br />Now of course the person you're going to think killed him would be his wife because the women basically boasted that she was going to kill him and how she would do it. But when he died she had an alibi which obviously made me suspicious as fuck.<br /><br />I feel like Agatha Christie loves to torture me because I was constantly guessing wrong. Like the entire fucking time. In almost every book. I would probably be the worst detective ever. I never saw the ending coming at all and I thoroughly enjoyed this book. It definitely got me back into the grove of her books and I'm so freaking pumped to read the next book!! </span>

<div id="review_91184042" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91184042" />
    <a title="Laurel Young" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3137295-laurel-young"><img alt="Laurel Young" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500435810p2/3137295.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/91184042?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laurel Young" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laurel Young" href="/user/show/3137295-laurel-young">Laurel Young</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3137295-laurel-young?shelf=my-year-of-agatha">my-year-of-agatha</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer91184042" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17605677637855639478">Absolutely classic Christie--this is the sort of novel that makes her reputation as Queen of Crime so richly deserved. I'd read it before and STILL got tangled in the red herrings and double, no triple, twists to the point that I questioned whether I had mis-remembered the solution. Poirot is at his best, and so is Hastings--we have to remember that Hastings, without meaning to be, is not a reliable narrator. He puts his own pedestrian spin on things: a suspect says something crucial, and Hastin</span>
  <span id="freeText17605677637855639478" style="display:none">Absolutely classic Christie--this is the sort of novel that makes her reputation as Queen of Crime so richly deserved. I'd read it before and STILL got tangled in the red herrings and double, no triple, twists to the point that I questioned whether I had mis-remembered the solution. Poirot is at his best, and so is Hastings--we have to remember that Hastings, without meaning to be, is not a reliable narrator. He puts his own pedestrian spin on things: a suspect says something crucial, and Hastings immediately mentally "explains" it to us. He is wrong, but we probably absorb his interpretation, which sounds so obvious and reasonable at the time. Poirot himself warns us when he tells Hastings that he is the perfect "average" man whom the murderer wishes to deceive--Hastings' conclusions are not stupid; they are average. To solve the case, we must be above-average like Poirot and take NOTHING for granted.<br /><br />This is also a wonderfully 1930s novel, with a heroine/prime suspect who is a film star in the mode of Gloria Swanson, Grace Kelly, and other classic Hollywood beauties. I love seeing how pop culture informs Dame Agatha's plots.<br /><br />Although this is classic Christie, it is also unusual in one regard: she tackles the surprising subject of a character who is into S&amp;M! She does it delicately and tastefully, but the meaning is plain. Lord Edgware is kinky! He reads the Marquis de Sade (for whom sadism --the "S" in "S&amp;M" --was named) and books on medieval torture. He has a butler who looks like "a Greek god", which seems to be why he was hired since he's a lousy butler. Lord Edgware is threatened with blackmail over his "unusual" proclivities. I've no idea why Christie decided to throw this in; perhaps she had just lately encountered the idea or it was in the press at the time or something. Anyway, it's not crucial to the plot but it does provide a creative, different motive for blackmail--something more spicy than the usual infidelity.<br /><br />Overall, inventive, puzzling, and first-rate.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17605677637855639478" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2015670738" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4791973222744057836">In <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/655140.Thirteen_at_Dinner' title='Thirteen at Dinner by Agatha Christie' rel='nofollow noopener'>Thirteen at Dinner</a> aka <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/215492.Lord_Edgware_Dies__Hercule_Poirot___9_' title='Lord Edgware Dies (Hercule Poirot, #9) by Agatha Christie' rel='nofollow noopener'>Lord Edgware Dies</a>, Lady Jane Edgware asks Hercule Poirot to rid her of her husband. He agrees to go see her husband and ask him to give her a divorce. That day Lord Edgware agrees to see Poirot and Hastings. According to Lord Edgware he has already written Lady Edgware agreeing to a divorce but Lady Jane claims never to have received the letter. That night Lord Edgware is murdered.<br/>Lady Jane is of course the prime suspect but she has an airtight alibi. At the time of the</span>
  <span id="freeText4791973222744057836" style="display:none">In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/655140.Thirteen_at_Dinner" title="Thirteen at Dinner by Agatha Christie" rel="nofollow noopener">Thirteen at Dinner</a> aka <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/215492.Lord_Edgware_Dies__Hercule_Poirot___9_" title="Lord Edgware Dies (Hercule Poirot, #9) by Agatha Christie" rel="nofollow noopener">Lord Edgware Dies</a>, Lady Jane Edgware asks Hercule Poirot to rid her of her husband. He agrees to go see her husband and ask him to give her a divorce. That day Lord Edgware agrees to see Poirot and Hastings. According to Lord Edgware he has already written Lady Edgware agreeing to a divorce but Lady Jane claims never to have received the letter. That night Lord Edgware is murdered.<br />Lady Jane is of course the prime suspect but she has an airtight alibi. At the time of the murder she is at a party with many witnesses.<br />As the book proceeds, every character takes a turn at being the prime suspect, but everyone has an alibi. Inspector Japp thinks that he's got his man and accuses Poirot of trying to complicate matters.<br />I have my suspect but that idea is quickly shot down. This is not my favorite Agatha Christie by any means. I liked it OK but found the constant shuffling of suspects to be confusing. Then suddenly Poirot solves the case. Maybe I need to take a break from Poirot.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('t3QqLH5OHnVo/dVTYrnnNBwRUm7Y27N2c23zbQZbxZML3NcJ165UlzFwhzEBz2rDfmFeJ6knaLUF3jslwivReQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('NdllufqYE25CH8qtPNNUOzXekFzASQSFpVcFlPJ4Us+JcZicU3hZjBuSmM9fpdnMV66cFbG130bT5M3cNghGJQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie">About Agatha Christie</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1589991473p3/123715.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie">Agatha Christie
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
42,813
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15007584132243146955"><i>Agatha Christie also wrote romance novels under the pseudonym <b><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16273767.Mary_Westmacott' title='Mary Westmacott' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mary Westmacott</a></b>, and was occasionally published under the name <b><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/19960421.Agatha_Christie_Mallowan' title='Agatha Christie Mallowan' rel='nofollow noopener'>Agatha Christie Mallowan</a></b>.</i><br/><br/>Agatha Christie is the best-selling author of all time. She wrote 66 crime novels and story collections, fourteen plays, and six novels under a pseudonym in Romance. Her books have sold over a billion copies in the English language and a billion in t</span>
  <span id="freeText15007584132243146955" style="display:none"><i>Agatha Christie also wrote romance novels under the pseudonym <b><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16273767.Mary_Westmacott" title="Mary Westmacott" rel="nofollow noopener">Mary Westmacott</a></b>, and was occasionally published under the name <b><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/19960421.Agatha_Christie_Mallowan" title="Agatha Christie Mallowan" rel="nofollow noopener">Agatha Christie Mallowan</a></b>.</i><br /><br />Agatha Christie is the best-selling author of all time. She wrote 66 crime novels and story collections, fourteen plays, and six novels under a pseudonym in Romance. Her books have sold over a billion copies in the English language and a billion in translation. According to Index Translationum, she remains the most-translated individual author, having been translated into at least 103 languages. She is the creator of two of the most enduring figures in crime literature-Hercule Poirot and Miss Jane Marple-and author of <i>The Mousetrap</i>, the longest-running play in the history of modern theatre. <br /><br />Agatha Mary Clarissa Miller was born in Torquay, Devon, England, U.K., as the youngest of three. The Millers had two other children: Margaret Frary Miller (1879–1950), called Madge, who was eleven years Agatha's senior, and Louis Montant Miller (1880–1929), called Monty, ten years older than Agatha. <br /><br />Before marrying and starting a family in London, she had served in a Devon hospital during the First World War, tending to troops coming back from the trenches. During the First World War, she worked at a hospital as a nurse; later working at a hospital pharmacy, a job that influenced her work, as many of the murders in her books are carried out with poison. During the Second World War, she worked as a pharmacy assistant at University College Hospital, London, acquiring a good knowledge of poisons which feature in many of her novels.<br /><br />Her first novel, <i>The Mysterious Affair at Styles</i>, came out in 1920. During her first marriage, Agatha published six novels, a collection of short stories, and a number of short stories in magazines.<br /><br />In late 1926, Agatha's husband, Archie, revealed that he was in love with another woman, Nancy Neele, and wanted a divorce. On 8 December 1926 the couple quarreled, and Archie Christie left their house, Styles, in Sunningdale, Berkshire, to spend the weekend with his mistress at Godalming, Surrey. That same evening Agatha disappeared from her home, leaving behind a letter for her secretary saying that she was going to Yorkshire. Her disappearance caused an outcry from the public, many of whom were admirers of her novels. Despite a massive manhunt, she was not found for eleven days.<br /><br />In 1930, Christie married archaeologist Max Mallowan (Sir Max from 1968) after joining him in an archaeological dig. Their marriage was especially happy in the early years and remained so until Christie's death in 1976.<br /><br />Christie frequently used familiar settings for her stories. Christie's travels with Mallowan contributed background to several of her novels set in the Middle East. Other novels (such as <i>And Then There Were None</i>) were set in and around Torquay, where she was born. Christie's 1934 novel <i>Murder on the Orient Express</i> was written in the Hotel Pera Palace in Istanbul, Turkey, the southern terminus of the railway. The hotel maintains Christie's room as a memorial to the author. The Greenway Estate in Devon, acquired by the couple as a summer residence in 1938, is now in the care of the National Trust.<br /><br />Christie often stayed at Abney Hall in Cheshire, which was owned by her brother-in-law, James Watts. She based at least two of her stories on the hall: the short story <i>The Adventure of the Christmas Pudding</i>, and the novel <i>After the Funeral</i>. Abney Hall became Agatha's greatest inspiration for country-house life, with all the servants and grandeur which have been woven into her plots.<br /><br /><br />To honour her many literary works, she was appointed Commander of the Order of the British Empire in the 1956 New Year Honours. The next year, she became the President of the Detection Club. <br /><br /><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agatha_Christie" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia entry for Agatha Christie</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15007584132243146955" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
